            <div class="catchall300" style='width:300px; overflow:hidden;float:right;'><!-- RoS_300x250 --> <div id ='div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13' style='width:300px; height:250px; overflow:hidden;'><script type='text/javascript'>if ($('#div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13').is(':visible')) {googletag.cmd.push(function () { googletag.display('div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13'); });}</script></div></div>

        <tr id="so4" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Dee Gordon earned a call up in early June and he performed much the way the Dodgers must have expected -- he hit for average, stole a lot of bases, and had absolutely zero power. In his 56 games as a major leaguer, he posted a .304/.325/.362 line with no home runs and 24 stolen bases. Speed is Gordon’s major weapon as he stole 73 bases in 2009 in Single-A ball and another 53 in 2010 at Double-A. If you combine his 2011, he stole a total of 56 bags. He’s never had power at any level, he’s not a terribly patient hitter, but he gets as much out of his game as anyone, putting nearly 56% of batted balls on the ground in an effort to put his wheels to work. He appears to be the logical starter and over a full season, he could be a real plus at steals and he should be able to hit for a decent average, but he may be a black hole at home runs and RBI. At shortstop, he still has good value. (Michael Barr)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Gordon has great speed and a starting gig. If he leads off for the Dodgers, they have a lineup that should give him the opportunity to score plenty of runs as well. While he’s going to have the power of Juan Pierre, as a shortstop, he’s still awfully valuable.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so5" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Blessed with electrifying speed, Gordon made enough of an impact in a 2011 cameo that the Dodgers gave him a starting job for 2012 and hoped his raw talent would make up for his inexperience. Unfortunately, it couldn't have gone worse in every possible way. <a href="http://www.fangraphs.com/leaders.aspx?pos=all&stats=bat&lg=all&qual=300&type=8&season=2012&month=0&season1=2012&ind=0&team=0&rost=0&age=0&filter=&players=0&sort=17,a">Only one player</a> with at least 300 plate appearances had a lower wOBA than his .253; on defense, <a href="http://www.fangraphs.com/leaders.aspx?pos=ss&stats=fld&lg=all&qual=650&type=1&season=2012&month=0&season1=2012&ind=0&team=0&rost=0&age=0&filter=&players=0&sort=24,a">no shortstop</a> with as much playing time had a worse UZR/150. To add injury to insult, his season essentially ended after injuring his thumb on July 3, and by the time he returned in September, Hanley Ramirez & Luis Cruz had frozen him out of a job. Only 25 in April, Gordon still has time to grow, but he's unlikely to be an everyday player in 2013. (<a href="http://www.twitter.com/mike_petriello">Mike Petriello</a>)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Dee Gordon is quite possibly the fastest man in the majors, but all that speed won't matter much if he can't learn to hit... or field... or stay healthy.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so6" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Thanks to Hanley Ramirez' continued injury woes, the Dodgers gave Gordon another shot to prove he could be a big league performer, and... oh, that did not go well. In 669 career plate appearances, or basically a full season, Gordon's line is a punchless .256/.301/.312. No amount of speed is going to make that okay, especially when it's an even lousier .229/.289/.285 over the last two seasons. Worse, Gordon's defense at shortstop is so awful that it's also not helping him make a case to stay in the lineup, and the team has started trying him out at second and in the outfield in a last-ditch attempt to reclaim some value. It's nice that his walk rate increased in a small sample, and Gordon's speed will always be appealing, but this is the definition of a one-tool player who can't take advantage of it due to a lack of all other tools. <a href="http://www.twitter.com/mike_petriello">(Mike Petriello)</a><br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>The potential for Gordon to be fantasy-eligible at multiple positions makes him slightly more appealing, but his bat is so weak that he's more likely to be in the minors than the bigs, making it difficult to recommend rostering him.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so7" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Last year at this time, Dee Gordon's career seemed to be nearly over. He'd flopped on both sides of the ball in parts of three seasons as the Dodger shortstop, and Alex Guerrero was imported from Cuba to play second base. In one of the most shocking turnarounds of the season, Gordon not only didn't spend the year in the minors, he held down the second base job from start to finish and even made an All-Star team, leading the National League in steals and playing adequate second base defense. The problem is that he walked only four times in the second half and watched his weighted runs created plus fall from a good first-half 113 to 84, without any change in batting average on balls in play. Still, he's at least earned himself the opportunity to show he can fix that, which is more than he had a year ago, and if he can get on base, having Giancarlo Stanton behind him to drive him in can't hurt. (<a href="http://www.twitter.com/mike_petriello">Mike Petriello</a>)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Gordon heads into 2015 all but assured of a starting job, and while he's a one-category performer, he's an elite base stealer -- assuming he can retain enough of 2014's improvement to continue to reach first base.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so8" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>In early drafts, Gordon’s average draft position has been sitting inside the top twenty. His .333 average and 60+ steal potential make him the class of the second base position along with Jose Altuve. Gordon likes to hit his way on, and that average was fueled by a .383 batting average on balls in play. He puts the ball on the ground at a 59.8% clip, and uses his legs to reach. In 2014, he reached base 42.6% of the time he laid down a bunt, and matched that in 2015 as bunts got him on base 42.1% of the time. It’s a weapon he deployed 38 times last year and he’s quite good at it. He’s very hard to pop up too, as his 3.8% infield fly ball rate was miniscule, especially for someone who you’d think would be pounded above the belt with heat. The majority of pitches he sees are down and away, and he’s not shy about pounding them into the ground and taking off. Pitchers aren’t worried about him driving the ball out of the park, and they send fastballs his way 66.5% of the time, well above the league average of 57.7%. When he swings at strikes, he connects at a 94.1% clip. He doesn’t mash anything, as his 85.5 mph exit velocity against fastballs isn’t Stanton-esque. He’s going to be atop a lineup that features Christian Yelich, Giancarlo Stanton, and Justin Bour, which is a mixed bag. He crossed the plate 88 times last year, and you can expect him to score around that much again.(Darren Schienbein)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Gordon is an elite fantasy second base, and with steals at a premium, you’ll be paying a price for Flash's kid. Don’t expect that .333 average to be repeatable, even with his speed. Steamer is looking at a return to .280ish, and there is nothing to suggest that that’s an unrealistic number.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so9" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Dee Gordon entered the 2016 season hoping to improve on his best year in the majors. In 2015, his first year with the Marlins, Gordon swiped a league-leading 58 bases to go along with a .333 average. Instead, Gordon's 2016 was defined by his 80-game suspension in late April for PED use. When he was on the field, Gordon continued his base-stealing ways, grabbing 30 in 79 games: in fact, Gordon succeeded on 81% of his steal attempts last year, an uptick from his 74% rate in 2015. On the other hand, Gordon's average fell to .268, the product of a BABIP drop from .383 to .319 -- and the xBABIP estimators suggest even this may have been a bit lucky, with a infield fly rate that doubled and a line drive rate that fell by 3 points. Don't expect the .330 average to return in 2017, but even batting .290 or so Gordon should still be a candidate to lead the league in steals. Between concerns over his post-PED production and the developing fantasy trend of deprioritizing speedsters, though, Gordon may end up being drafted as a 2B10 or so -- where he would be very likely to bring solid fantasy value. (Brice Russ)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>After a career year in 2015, Dee Gordon's average fell 65 points -- and he was suspended half the season for PEDs, too. Don't expect a complete rebound in AVG, but his game-changing SB potential still makes him a top-10 second baseman to target.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
    </tr>

                <div id="SeasonStats1_tsStats" class="RadTabStrip RadTabStrip_FanGraphs RadTabStripTop_FanGraphs RadTabStripTop">
